    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

<span id="freeTextContainer16354041973074093801"><i>"No woman in the three-hundred-year history of the karyukai has ever come forward in public to tell her story. We have been constrained by unwritten rules not to do so, by the robes of tradition and by the sanctity of our exclusive calling...But I feel it is time to speak out."</i><br/><br/>Celebrated as the most successful geisha of her generation, Mineko Iwasaki was only five years ol</span>
  <span id="freeText16354041973074093801" style="display:none"><i>"No woman in the three-hundred-year history of the karyukai has ever come forward in public to tell her story. We have been constrained by unwritten rules not to do so, by the robes of tradition and by the sanctity of our exclusive calling...But I feel it is time to speak out."</i><br /><br />Celebrated as the most successful geisha of her generation, Mineko Iwasaki was only five years old when she left her parents' home for the world of the geisha. For the next twenty-five years, she would live a life filled with extraordinary professional demands and rich rewards. She would learn the formal customs and language of the geisha, and study the ancient arts of Japanese dance and music. She would enchant kings and princes, captains of industry, and titans of the entertainment world, some of whom would become her dearest friends. Through great pride and determination, she would be hailed as one of the most prized geishas in Japan's history, and one of the last great practitioners of this now fading art form.<br /><br />In <i>Geisha, a Life</i>, Mineko Iwasaki tells her story, from her warm early childhood, to her intense yet privileged upbringing in the Iwasaki okiya (household), to her years as a renowned geisha, and finally, to her decision at the age of twenty-nine to retire and marry, a move that would mirror the demise of geisha culture. Mineko brings to life the beauty and wonder of Gion Kobu, a place that "existed in a world apart, a special realm whose mission and identity depended on preserving the time-honored traditions of the past." She illustrates how it coexisted within post-World War II Japan at a time when the country was undergoing its radical transformation from a post-feudal society to a modern one.<br /><br />"There is much mystery and misunderstanding about what it means to be a geisha. I hope this story will help explain what it is really like and also serve as a record of this unique component of Japan's cultural history," writes Mineko Iwasaki. <i>Geisha, a Life</i> is the first of its kind, as it delicately unfolds the fabric of a geisha's development. Told with great wisdom and sensitivity, it is a true story of beauty and heroism, and of a time and culture rarely revealed to the Western world.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16354041973074093801" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>
</div>

Popular Answered Questions

<div id='communityQuestionsWithAnswers'>
<div class='communityQuestionAndAnswer'>
<div class='communityQuestion'>
<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/679406-how-certain-can-we-be-that-what-mineko">How certain can we be that what Mineko wrote in the novel about herself is true? A. Golden’s Memoirs of a Geisha is subjected to such scrutiny even though it is historical fiction, not historical fact, but a former geisha’s claim to be the greatest of her time isn’t? Why’s that?</a>
</div>
<div class='communityQAFooter questionFooter'>
<ul class='questionFooterActions'>
<li class='footerItem like'>
<span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_community_question_679406'><a id="like_count_community_question_679406" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/679406?resource_type=CommunityQuestion"><span class="likesCount">2 likes</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F522534.Geisha_a_Life">like</a></span>

</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionTimestamp'>
<a href="/questions/679406-how-certain-can-we-be-that-what-mineko">4 years ago</a>
</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionPermalink'>
<a href="/questions/679406-how-certain-can-we-be-that-what-mineko">See all 8 answers</a>
</li>
</ul>
</div>

</div>
<div class='allCommunityAnswers'>
<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/21322837-heather-mcalister"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1484601838i/21322837._UX100_CR0,0,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/21322837-heather-mcalister">Heather McAlister</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">White male privilege at its finest: Golden can interview a woman from another country and culture from his own about her own life, mess around with th<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">White male privilege at its finest: Golden can interview a woman from another country and culture from his own about her own life, mess around with the details to make his story seem more spicy, lie to her about keeping her anonymous and then publish her name to give his own writing more credibility anyway, and people still assume that his word is more trustworthy than hers. Iwasaki Mineko is just a woman born and raised in Japan, who joined the geiko when she was a small child in the 1940's, and spent decades working as a geiko in Gion; why would her account be more credible than Aruthur Golden's? A straight white male American professor who it's doubtful ever set foot in Japan?<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>
</div>

<div id="review_24447247" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/24447247" />

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/24447247?book_show_action=true">Jun 13, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Petra-X" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Petra-X" href="/user/show/1237196-petra-x">Petra-X</a>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer24447247" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5690110509190978684">The book, Memoirs of a Geisha by Arthur Golden was based around interviews with Mineko Iwasaki. She was unhappy with the misuse of her words and wrote this, her autobiography. The book details her life as a geisha from childhood up until her retirement a few years ago, in her 40s. <br/><br/>In the West, at least, 'geisha' has always been thought of as a euphemism for a high-priced whore, but as the book shows, the women earn far more as geishas than they could ever hope to do on their backs. The world of </span>
  <span id="freeText5690110509190978684" style="display:none">The book, Memoirs of a Geisha by Arthur Golden was based around interviews with Mineko Iwasaki. She was unhappy with the misuse of her words and wrote this, her autobiography. The book details her life as a geisha from childhood up until her retirement a few years ago, in her 40s. <br /><br />In the West, at least, 'geisha' has always been thought of as a euphemism for a high-priced whore, but as the book shows, the women earn far more as geishas than they could ever hope to do on their backs. The world of a geisha is one where women run things and make lots of money whilst all the time looking like the epitome of sweet, submissive feminity.</span>

<div id="review_677877244" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/677877244" />
    <a title="Madeline" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/111921-madeline"><img alt="Madeline" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1220803522p2/111921.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/677877244?book_show_action=true">Jul 26, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Madeline" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Madeline" href="/user/show/111921-madeline">Madeline</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/111921-madeline?shelf=memoir">memoir</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer677877244" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5652301362145292697">"No woman in the three-hundred year history of the karyukai has ever come forward in public to tell her story. We have been constrained by unwritten rules not to do so, by the robes of tradition, and by the sanctity of our exclusive calling. <br/>But I feel it is time to speak out. I want you to know what it is really like to live the life of a geisha, a life filled with extraordinary professional demands and richly glorious rewards. Many say I was the best geisha of my generation; I was certainly th</span>
  <span id="freeText5652301362145292697" style="display:none">"No woman in the three-hundred year history of the karyukai has ever come forward in public to tell her story. We have been constrained by unwritten rules not to do so, by the robes of tradition, and by the sanctity of our exclusive calling. <br />But I feel it is time to speak out. I want you to know what it is really like to live the life of a geisha, a life filled with extraordinary professional demands and richly glorious rewards. Many say I was the best geisha of my generation; I was certainly the most successful. And yet, it was a life that I found too constrictive to continue. And one that I ultimately had to leave. <br />It is a story I have long wanted to tell."<br /><br />We all remember <i>Memoirs of a Geisha</i>, right? You know, that book where a white American dude decided that he was the best candidate for writing a story about the secretive, all-female world of the Japanese geisha? Remember how well that worked out? Full disclosure: I really liked that book for much longer than I should have (I remember hearing that they were making a movie version and being really invested in who they cast), and it wasn't until I was in college that I learned some unpleasant truths about the creation of this book. For his book, Arthur Golden conducted a lot of interviews with a retired geisha, which formed the basis for the story. When the book came out, this geisha was so horrified at the way Golden had twisted her words to fit his Western worldview of the geisha that she wrote her <i>own</i> memoir in response. That geisha, as you can guess, was Mineko Iwasaki, and this book is the real <i>Memoirs of a Geisha</i>. No fetishization, no male gaze, no bullshit.<br /><br />Iwasaki was a geisha (she refers to herself as a geiko, a more specific term used in the area of Kyoto where she lived and trained) starting in the 1960's, and was easily the most successful of her time - a feat which she accomplished by taking every single appointment available, not taking a single day off for five years, and sleeping three hours every night. She retired at the age of twenty-nine because, as she says in her introduction, the lifestyle eventually grew too restrictive and her efforts to implement change were ignored. <br /><br />The book starts with her childhood, when she was three years old and the owner of an okiya first started trying to recruit her. Iwasaki spent her childhood living in the okiya as a sort of boarding school (it was a super weird situation, honestly, because her parents were allowed to visit but barely saw her, and also she was five) before she ultimately made the decision to be adopted by the okiya owner and live there full-time at the age of seven. From then on, Iwasaki worked full-time training to be a geiko before making her debut at age fifteen. <br /><br />The detail is extensive: Iwasaki's favorite aspect of training was dance, so we learn a lot about Japanese styles of dance, and every other part of the journey from apprentice to full-time geiko. Everything is described in great (but often slightly clinical) detail, and it's worth it purely for the time Iwasaki spends describing every part of a geiko's outfit, from shoes to hair ornaments, and the kimonos she describes are so gorgeous it'll make your mouth water. There's also a lot of practical information, like this bit about how geikos' wages are calculated:<br /><br />"At the end of the night, the ochaya calculates the hanadai for all the maiko and geiko who have attended banquets there that evening. They write the tallies down on slips of paper that they place in a box in the entryway of the ochaya. The next morning a representative of the <i>kenban</i>, or financial affairs office, makes the rounds of the ochaya to collect all the slips from the night before. These are tallied and reported to the Kabukai. The kenban is an independent organization that performs this service on behalf of the geiko association."<br /><br />She also puts to rest, once and for all, the misconception that geisha are just fancy prostitutes. Remember that horrifying part in <i>Memoirs of a Geisha</i> where Sayuri's virginity is sold off to the highest bidder in a ceremony called a <i>mizuage</i>? I don't have the space to recount all the ways that's wrong - you'll have to just read the book and let Iwasaki explain why Arthur Golden is an asshole. Instead, I'll let her explain where Golden got the idea for that scene (spoiler alert! it wasn't from the geisha):<br /><br />"Shimabara used to be a licensed quarter where women known as <i>oiran</i> and <i>tayu</i> (courtesans, high-class prostitutes) plied their trade, though they were accomplished in the traditional arts as well. A young oiran also underwent a ritual called a "mizuage" but hers consisted of being ceremoniously deflowered by a patron who had paid handsomely for the privilege."<br /><br />Hey, Arthur Golden? If you want to write a book that proves geisha aren't prostitutes, maybe don't include a scene where the geisha <i>has sex for money</i>, especially if that little detail is a complete fabrication.<br /><br />My only big complaint about this book is the writing itself. I'm willing the blame the dry, plodding prose on the translation; less easy to excuse is the lack of transitions and topic sentences. Iwasaki will be describing a dance class, and then in the next paragraph will have moved on to a completely different subject with no warning or explanation, and it was irritating. She writes in the Q&amp;A at the end of the book that it took her four months to write, which I definitely believe.<br /><br />But these are minor quibbles. This is a good book, if for no other reason than it's a fantastic primary source into a fascinating and misunderstood world. We should all be proud of Iwasaki for resisting the urge to go with the original title, <i>Arthur Golden Can Suck It</i>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5652301362145292697" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_410216946" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/410216946" />
    <a title="Kara" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/284485-kara"><img alt="Kara" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1467408906p2/284485.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/410216946?book_show_action=true">Sep 08, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kara" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kara" href="/user/show/284485-kara">Kara</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780743430593" href="/book/show/935.Geisha_of_Gion">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/284485-kara?shelf=memoir">memoir</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/284485-kara?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer410216946" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10869793503110965888">This is Iwasaki's response to <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/930.Memoirs_of_a_Geisha' title='Memoirs of a Geisha by Arthur Golden' rel='nofollow noopener'>Memoirs of a Geisha</a>. I picked this up because I thought it'd be great to get the truth behind the story. This fell flat. <br/><br/>The book couldn't decide if it was a memoir or a history of geisha in post-war Kyoto. If a history, it lacked description, and the author inserted too much of her annoying self (more on this later) into the story. If a memoir, the author didn't talk enough about her emotions. For example, she tries to kill herself as a young girl, and the descript</span>
  <span id="freeText10869793503110965888" style="display:none">This is Iwasaki's response to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/930.Memoirs_of_a_Geisha" title="Memoirs of a Geisha by Arthur Golden" rel="nofollow noopener">Memoirs of a Geisha</a>. I picked this up because I thought it'd be great to get the truth behind the story. This fell flat. <br /><br />The book couldn't decide if it was a memoir or a history of geisha in post-war Kyoto. If a history, it lacked description, and the author inserted too much of her annoying self (more on this later) into the story. If a memoir, the author didn't talk enough about her emotions. For example, she tries to kill herself as a young girl, and the description plus the emotional turmoil she was feeling took up all of three sentences. Yes, three sentences. Iwasaki tried to make this part memoir and part history, and she ended up with poor excuses for either.<br /><br />There was also something a little off with the description. I don't know if this was the fault of the translation or Iwasaki herself. Here's a passage: "The shamisen is a three-stringed instrument that is played like a viola." I play shamisen. Besides the fact that is has strings and is vaguely shaped like a viola, it's nothing like a viola. You pick it rather than bow it, and it's held like a guitar rather than a viola. I have no idea where this description came from.<br /><br />But what really, really bugged me about this book is the author's <i>ridiculous</i> arrogance. The bulk of the novel is spent discussing how beautiful, talented, and loved she is. She's everyone's favorite except for the people who are just jealous of how wonderful she is. She's gifted at everything she tries: from dance to basketball. She takes this to such a ridiculous level that, at one point, she insists that she doesn't fart. Yes, you read that right. Here's the passage:<blockquote>The consultant came to examine me and asked if I had passed gas.<br />"Gas?" I asked.<br />"Yes, gas. Has any come out yet?"<br />"Come out? From where?"<br />"What I mean is, have you broken wind? Have you farted?"<br />"Excuse me," I replied indignantly. "I don't do things like that."</blockquote><br />Apparently, biological rules do not apply to Iwasaki.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10869793503110965888" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer16599186161846513493">This woman wrote her book in a response to Memoirs of a Geisha because she felt that the book gave the wrong impression. Unfortunately for readers, this book is story after story about how great and important the author was / is. It doesn't represent life as a geisha, it represents life seeking fame.</span>

<div id="review_2368547645" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2368547645" />
    <a title="☘Misericordia☘ ~ The Serendipity Aegis ~  ⚡ϟ⚡ϟ⚡⛈ ✺❂❤❣" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6603759-misericordia-the-serendipity-aegis"><img alt="☘Misericordia☘ ~ The Serendipity Aegis ~  ⚡ϟ⚡ϟ⚡⛈ ✺❂❤❣" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1585764390p2/6603759.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2368547645?book_show_action=true">Apr 21, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3570431-elizabeth?shelf=memoir-autobiography">memoir-autobiography</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3570431-elizabeth?shelf=own-read">own-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3570431-elizabeth?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3570431-elizabeth?shelf=5-stars">5-stars</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3570431-elizabeth?shelf=poc-woc">poc-woc</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3570431-elizabeth?shelf=2015">2015</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer740084284" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16583648751471772609">First, I would like to urge anyone who wants to learn more about geisha - READ THIS BOOK INSTEAD OF MEMOIRS OF A GEISHA. The author of that, Arthur Golden, interviewed Mineko Iwasaki and twisted her tales into falsities, making it seem that geisha were high class prostitutes. This is not the case - oiran, a high class courtesan, sold their bodies, not geisha. In fact, Iwasaki was extremely upset when she realized Golden had twisted her facts on the life of being a geisha, and decided to write he</span>
  <span id="freeText16583648751471772609" style="display:none">First, I would like to urge anyone who wants to learn more about geisha - READ THIS BOOK INSTEAD OF MEMOIRS OF A GEISHA. The author of that, Arthur Golden, interviewed Mineko Iwasaki and twisted her tales into falsities, making it seem that geisha were high class prostitutes. This is not the case - oiran, a high class courtesan, sold their bodies, not geisha. In fact, Iwasaki was extremely upset when she realized Golden had twisted her facts on the life of being a geisha, and decided to write her factual and realistic account. Her memoir reads as a beautiful balance between her personal recollections and facts on geisha (or the term used specifically in Kyoto, geiko) life in the 60s-70s. I expected a pretty basic factual account, but was pleasantly surprised by Mineko's escapades - hiding in the closet as a kid, working her hardest to embrace her passion for dancing, chasing down the pervy men who harassed her. Put straightforward, Mineko Iwasaki is a bad ass, and I would love to meet her one day if possible. I highly recommend this memoir for anyone interested in personal stories, the lives of geisha or how Japanese society functioned in the 1950s through 1970s. 4.5/5 stars.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16583648751471772609" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_556952323" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/556952323" />
    <a title="Ingrid Lola" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1852710-ingrid-lola"><img alt="Ingrid Lola" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1334330590p2/1852710.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/556952323?book_show_action=true">Mar 09, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ingrid Lola" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ingrid Lola" href="/user/show/1852710-ingrid-lola">Ingrid Lola</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1852710-ingrid-lola?shelf=unintentionally-funny">unintentionally-funny</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer556952323" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2839771105679062479">Yeeah ... Mineko Iwasaki unfortunately comes off as very unlikeable in this book. The overtone that she is trying to prove something (that Arthur Golden was "wrong" [even though he was writing fiction, which I feel she should understand, since she knows everything about art and all?]) is very, very strong. Like way too strong. Like it kind of made me laugh. It just didn't read well at all. <br/><br/>I would love to have read more about how Mineko challenged the system (like she claims she did, but never s</span>
  <span id="freeText2839771105679062479" style="display:none">Yeeah ... Mineko Iwasaki unfortunately comes off as very unlikeable in this book. The overtone that she is trying to prove something (that Arthur Golden was "wrong" [even though he was writing fiction, which I feel she should understand, since she knows everything about art and all?]) is very, very strong. Like way too strong. Like it kind of made me laugh. It just didn't read well at all. <br /><br />I would love to have read more about how Mineko challenged the system (like she claims she did, but never says exactly how) instead of about how amazing she was and that everybody loved her and that these the way Arthur Golden portrayed geisha in Memoirs of a Geisha is WRONG. (Even though the ambiguity and mystery of geisha is part of their aesthetic... I wish she would have gone into this more.) <br /><br />Even from the first few chapters I wasn't sure if I believed that her account is really "true." It just feels so off. Like, she keeps repeating that she insisted on not drinking sake until she was at drinking age, with lots of strange side stories to back this up, and the way-too-detailed and uninteresting story of how she lost her virginity to a man she truly loved of her own free will, and it was wild and beautiful and perfect, and blabla...which was obviously in there to challenge Arthur Golden's statement that she told him her virginity was auctioned for the equivalent of $850,000, which he also claims he has on tapes he recorded when he interviewed her for his book in 1992. Strange.<br /><br />Anyway. It was way too stilted to feel like a real, genuine account to me. Even if it is, her tone and presentation made it feel like it wasn't. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="2839771105679062479" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_428152901" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/428152901" />
    <a title="Cheryl" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11255849-cheryl"><img alt="Cheryl" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1565547225p2/11255849.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/428152901?book_show_action=true">Oct 05, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cheryl" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cheryl" href="/user/show/11255849-cheryl">Cheryl</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Cheryl by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/2990642-rowena">Rowena</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11255849-cheryl?shelf=global-intrigue">global-intrigue</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11255849-cheryl?shelf=asia">asia</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer428152901" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15228351262971926433">I started reading this as a memoir and realized my mistake because I was yearning for more emotion, more of an understanding of the narrator. I should have been reading it as an autobiography instead though, because it certainly has the texture of the traditional autobiography (rumors are, it was ghost-written). There is a lot here about the Japanese culture and the pictures really help you place the descriptions.<br/><br/>Mineko Iwasaki tells the story of her life as a geisha in Japan. Written after the </span>
  <span id="freeText15228351262971926433" style="display:none">I started reading this as a memoir and realized my mistake because I was yearning for more emotion, more of an understanding of the narrator. I should have been reading it as an autobiography instead though, because it certainly has the texture of the traditional autobiography (rumors are, it was ghost-written). There is a lot here about the Japanese culture and the pictures really help you place the descriptions.<br /><br />Mineko Iwasaki tells the story of her life as a geisha in Japan. Written after the famous <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/930.Memoirs_of_a_Geisha" title="Memoirs of a Geisha by Arthur Golden" rel="nofollow noopener">Memoirs of a Geisha</a>, there is much speculation (even an alleged lawsuit) about the nonfiction being written as a rebuttal to the novel. In an online interview with Golden about his novel, it is alleged that he had a fax that shows how the geisha asked him to put her face out there more, which leads you to believe that once the book became a hit with millions of dollars, she wanted more. Golden created composite characters and different settings and scenarios so that the novel could read like fiction, thus honoring the protection-of-privacy deal he'd had with the real-life geisha. In the end, she seemed to dislike the fictional portrayal. <br /> <br />What I didn't like was the anger and chiding that loomed throughout the autobiography. The author wanted people to know that geishas are not what they seem. Yes, we get it. It was the way in which she wrote it though, that was off-putting: "We are de facto diplomats who have to be able to communicate with anyone. But this doesn't mean we are doormats." Statements like this wouldn't have been necessary to say had she just shown it. <br /><br />And then there is the chiding: "This is why the whole notion of geisha houses being dens of ill repute is so ridiculous. Men are barely allowed inside..." There were not a lot of scenes of the geisha entertainment; instead, the author and her translator chose to focus on the dancing and dance lessons. Besides talking about her long-term relationship with a married man and some meetings with royalty, she doesn't really go into a lot about the entertaining.<br /><br />After reading Arthur Golden's well-written, <i>Memoirs of a Geisha,</i> and feeling some sympathy for the orphan girl forced into that life, reading this true story was a bit difficult, since the real geisha insists that it was her choice, at five years old, to leave her parents, that she could visit them at any time, and that she had the upper hand at her geisha house. Really? </span>
  <a data-text-id="15228351262971926433" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Claudia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/20498957-claudia"><img alt="Claudia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1507465969p2/20498957.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1223973317?book_show_action=true">Aug 27, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Claudia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Claudia" href="/user/show/20498957-claudia">Claudia</a>
      </span>

          marked it as dnf-not-my-cup-of-coffee


          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/6622690-geisha">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20498957-claudia?shelf=land-of-the-rising-sun">land-of-the-rising-sun</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20498957-claudia?shelf=shreds-of-life">shreds-of-life</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20498957-claudia?shelf=utterly-disappointing">utterly-disappointing</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20498957-claudia?shelf=auto-biography-memoir">auto-biography-memoir</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1223973317" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13936837567242609988"><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/929.Memoirs_of_a_Geisha' title='Memoirs of a Geisha by Arthur Golden' rel='nofollow noopener'>Memoirs of a Geisha</a> is based on the life of Mineko Iwasaki, the author of this book. While the former is fiction, this one is the true account of her life.<br/><br/>Being an autobiography, I expected to be credible, but when I read such passages, my trust in what follows fades into inexistence:<br/><br/><i>"The lady looked at me for a second. Her body was very still but I saw her eyes widen.[...] "You know, Mr. Tanaka, I have been looking for an atotori [...] for a very long time and I have the oddest sensation that I</i></span>
  <span id="freeText13936837567242609988" style="display:none"><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/929.Memoirs_of_a_Geisha" title="Memoirs of a Geisha by Arthur Golden" rel="nofollow noopener">Memoirs of a Geisha</a> is based on the life of Mineko Iwasaki, the author of this book. While the former is fiction, this one is the true account of her life.<br /><br />Being an autobiography, I expected to be credible, but when I read such passages, my trust in what follows fades into inexistence:<br /><br /><i>"The lady looked at me for a second. Her body was very still but I saw her eyes widen.[...] "You know, Mr. Tanaka, I have been looking for an atotori [...] for a very long time and I have the oddest sensation that I may have just found her." I had no idea what she was talking about. I didn't know what an atotori was or why she needed one. <b>But I felt the energy in her body change.</b>"</i> This at the age of 3. Wow...<br /><br />There are others like this and all the introspection, clear and vivid memories, the perspicacity to read one's emotions, not to mention taking her destiny into her own hands until/at the age of 5, made me roll my eyes way too often.<br /><br />Add to this a very dull writing (or maybe it's the translation, I can't tell) and the result is an incredulous and bored me abandoning the book at 23%.<br /><br />As much as I loved Golden's book years ago, this was utterly dissapointing.</span>

<div id="review_3184023139" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3184023139" />
    <a title="B. P. Rinehart" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4635205-b-p-rinehart"><img alt="B. P. Rinehart" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1347740735p2/4635205.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3184023139?book_show_action=true">Feb 19, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="B. P. Rinehart" class="user" itemprop="url" name="B. P. Rinehart" href="/user/show/4635205-b-p-rinehart">B. P. Rinehart</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780743444323" href="/book/show/1430747.Geisha_a_Life">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4635205-b-p-rinehart?shelf=biography">biography</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4635205-b-p-rinehart?shelf=history-stuff">history-stuff</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4635205-b-p-rinehart?shelf=non-fiction-stuff">non-fiction-stuff</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3184023139" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16562161508452557104">This was an interesting read and I read it in an interesting time. Right after a controversy breaks-out over a novel that is written sensationalizing the lives of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/376919.By_the_Lake_of_Sleeping_Children' title='By the Lake of Sleeping Children by Luis Alberto Urrea' rel='nofollow noopener'>real people</a>, I read this book which was written in answer to an author who got rich writing a sensationalized (i. e. false) version of the experiences of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/617.Mineko_Iwasaki' title='Mineko Iwasaki' rel='nofollow noopener'>Mineko Iwasaki</a>. I'd owned this book for over a year, but did not know when I was going to be able to read it. I thought now was as good a time as any. Since I got some other ground to</span>
  <span id="freeText16562161508452557104" style="display:none">This was an interesting read and I read it in an interesting time. Right after a controversy breaks-out over a novel that is written sensationalizing the lives of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/376919.By_the_Lake_of_Sleeping_Children" title="By the Lake of Sleeping Children by Luis Alberto Urrea" rel="nofollow noopener">real people</a>, I read this book which was written in answer to an author who got rich writing a sensationalized (i. e. false) version of the experiences of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/617.Mineko_Iwasaki" title="Mineko Iwasaki" rel="nofollow noopener">Mineko Iwasaki</a>. I'd owned this book for over a year, but did not know when I was going to be able to read it. I thought now was as good a time as any. Since I got some other ground to cover I'll deal with this here: Geisha are not prostitutes. Period. They go about sex just like the rest of us: messily. In Japan they had courtesans called <i>oiran</i> who learned traditional dance and song, but who's main goal was 'hoing. Geisha are at best hostess, but sex ain't part of the regular job, it's the woman's own choice who she sleeps with. This book was written to be a refutation of the white fetishization of a certain novel, but I think it has a lot more going for it and I wish to talk more about it in this review. <br /><br />Mineko Iwasaki was born Masaka Tanaka to Japanese nobles down on their luck after prettey much everything that had occurred in Japan between the Meji Restoration and the end of WWII. Her family's choice to stay in Japan after the imperial family decided to move their court from Kyoto to Tokyo saw their Noble rank be downgraded. During The Great Depression more hard times hit the family and young Mineko's father decides to sell some of his daughters to an equally struggling okiya (geisha house) called Iwasaki, which was a satellite house of a larger okiya. After WWII, Masako/Mineko is born and her family eventually recovers--unfortunately the Iwasaki okiya does not. Her father is determined that his last daughter not become a geisha, and the woman running the okiya is equally determine that Masako become the heir to okiya. By this time, the post-war Japanese government has introduced legislation banning prostitution (formally ending the oiran trade) and also debt bondage which ends the practice of indentured geisha. Unfortunately for Masako's father, one can still be a geisha by choice and our protagonist is artistically inclined and a natural prodigy of dance. Masako ends up choosing to join of her own free will despite being an introvert and independent-minded (two things that are the complete opposite of what a geisha is suppose to be). She joins at the age of 5, and of the last generation of girls who can become geisha before reform laws make junior high/middle school graduation compulsory before joining the "flower and willow world." She eventually is formally adopted by the okiya and her name is changed to Mineko Iwasaki (I will be referring to her as Mineko since one of the women who adopted her is also called Masako), and she goes on to become the most iconic maiko (junior geisha) and geiko (senior geisha) of the post-war. <br /><br />The distinction of a junior geisha as a maiko is only in Kyoto, geisha in other parts of Japan are simply called geiko (woman of art) or geisha (person of art--the term originally applied to itinerant <b>male</b> musicians around Japan). In Kyoto their is a whole infrastructure that is devoted to this industry. Part of the conflict that this book is <i>really</i> about is about how the division of labor is practiced in this industry. If I tried to explain how this whole thing worked it would be too long, but that's why you read the book for because Mineko and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/3177.Rande_Brown" title="Rande Brown" rel="nofollow noopener">Rande Brown</a> do a good job. But I will come back to why Mineko quit the job at the height of her popularity.<br /><br />Talking specifically about the book, the format and actual story called back two books for me: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8683999.David_Copperfield" title="David Copperfield by Charles Dickens" rel="nofollow noopener">David Copperfield</a> &amp; <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18625133.Twelve_Years_a_Slave" title="Twelve Years a Slave by Solomon Northup" rel="nofollow noopener">Twelve Years a Slave</a>. The book has a very 19th century memoir feel to it and I don't if that is Brown's academic rendering or Minkeo's traditionalist education. For me it gave the book an interesting feel as the tone and setting felt a century apart--I was intrigued by it. The way the people and atmosphere of this book is presented does feel very Dickensian so if you like Charles Dickens, you should like this book. You fall in love with the good characters, and you despise the bad ones. Now, given the initail mission statement of this book, it assumes you know nothing about geisha, Japanese history, or have been misinformed about both, so the book teaches you in-depth on everything you need to know about this world. This is exactly what <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/96426.Solomon_Northup" title="Solomon Northup" rel="nofollow noopener">Solomon Northup</a> did regarding his intended audiences knowledge of American Slavery. This a great beginners guide to this subject and it makes the title of the book fit perfectly.<br /><br />When I think of how Mineko Iwasaki became the geisha of her generation, it seems that despite her being very much of the post-way Showa generation, she was raised and instilled with the sensibilities and work ethic of people born in the earlier generations (i. e. Meji and Taisho), so it was natural for her to be such a hard and grueling worker. Combine that with her 1st love of dance and it made her a natural fit for success despite her equally natural disdain for partying and people. I mean, she took no days off for 5 years. There is no secret to how she became number 1. Mineko benefits from having some powerful people in her corner at all times (it really is who you know...) and from operating in the top Geisha district in Japan Gion Kobu. She ends up entertaining and knowing numerous heads of state, politicians, artists, and business folks from Japan and around the world. She relates some very wild stories about that other imperial family the Windsors (let's just say she could've warned Megan Markle) and she seemed to prefer artists and academics to all other clients--the father of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/259401.Shuntar__Tanikawa" title="Shuntarō Tanikawa" rel="nofollow noopener">Shuntarō Tanikawa</a> became a surrogate father to her and she struck-up a love affair with the actor <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shintaro_Katsu" rel="nofollow noopener">Shintaro Katsu</a>. <br /><br />But of course there were cons. The two crazy things I learned was the intense amount of jealousy and hazing in this industry. All but the most senior geiko were vicious to her on her way to the top. The worst offender was her pure evil oldest sister who was also a geisha. Yaeko ranks among the top of most villainous women in world literature (to say nothing of her son...). When it was not hazing by others, it was the crazy harassment from men in general that she had to endure. Mostly on the street, but also some of her clients would make sure to make the case for a #MeToo movement being necessary. Thank god for taxis. <br /><br />"<i>So we support the dance but it does not support us.</i>"<br /><br />The thing that ultimately causes her to quit and close the Iwasaki okiya was not any sexual exploitation, but economic exploitation. Though the geisha go out and bring in the clientele and do the hard work, they get the least amount of money in their industry. The schools and businesses around them profit the most and Mineko at the height of her success was not allowed to make any money using her artistic skills for her own profit. She wasn't even allowed to teach in the dance school that she learned her dances in despite being the best dancer there. She realized that a very small group of people were getting a large amount of the millions of dollars being made in Gion Kobu and her and most of her peers were not even being compensated with proper worker's compensation, post-junior high school education, or a pension system for older geisha. She spent a decade complaining to the authorities responsible, but they dismissed her concerns. She thought if she quit and got other geisha to defect with her it would cause a crisis that would force reform, but she underestimated the inherent inability that Japanese society had to adapting to the changing times and nothing has really changed in the Gion since she left other than the decrease in numbers of people who can teach these arts properly. <br /><br />She retired in 1980, started a family with a painter, and became a patroness of the arts in Japan. It was not until some random white man wanted to write a paperback romance featuring geisha (and botched it) that she was drawn back to "<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/40265834.The_Source_of_Self_Regard_Selected_Essays__Speeches__and_Meditations" title="The Source of Self-Regard Selected Essays, Speeches, and Meditations by Toni Morrison" rel="nofollow noopener">the site of memory</a>" and eventually wrote this book. <br /><br /><br />If you want further recommendations on this subject (because I know you GR folks hate to read something and get smart) I got two movies to recommend. A very hard to find tv movie adaptation of this book exist called <a href="https://www.imdb.com/title/tt11503012/" rel="nofollow noopener">Flower Battle (or Hanaikusa)</a> which was made in 2007. This movie is almost impossible to watch with subtitles unless you can internet very well. An easier movie to find which I have actually watched is <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/A_Geisha" rel="nofollow noopener">A Geisha (1953)</a> directed by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/5272881.Kenji_Mizoguchi" title="Kenji Mizoguchi" rel="nofollow noopener">Kenji Mizoguchi</a> which looks at a maiko and geiko in the immediate post-war Japan and was made by someone who has a very complicated pre-war history with the "flower and willow world." <br />There is also this youtube channel: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.youtube.com/user/1113kunihiko">https://www.youtube.com/user/1113kuni...</a> which seems to be from somebody who works in the geisha industry. I was able to corroborate many of Mineko Iwasaki's descriptions of the different dances and even the details of how maiko and geiko dress from just browsing through these videos.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16562161508452557104" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2250632386" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2250632386" />
    <a title="Ailsa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6117156-ailsa"><img alt="Ailsa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442286271p2/6117156.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2250632386?book_show_action=true">Jan 09, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ailsa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ailsa" href="/user/show/6117156-ailsa">Ailsa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780743220361" href="/book/show/531858.Geisha_of_Gion">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2250632386" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1143820707365322510"><b>"I believed that self-discipline was the key to beauty."</b><br/><br/>Mineko Iwasaki successfully sued Arthur Golden for modelling his novel <i>Memoirs of a Geisha </i>on her life. I can't really blame him. This memoir gets three stars solely due to how fascinating it is to see what it takes to become the most successful Geiko* of a generation.**<br/><br/><i>Geisha of Gion</i> is far from a perfect read. My gripes, in no particular order:<br/><br/>1) Other reviewers suggest that Iwasaki had a ghost writer. If she did, she was robbed. There wa</span>
  <span id="freeText1143820707365322510" style="display:none"><b>"I believed that self-discipline was the key to beauty."</b><br /><br />Mineko Iwasaki successfully sued Arthur Golden for modelling his novel <i>Memoirs of a Geisha </i>on her life. I can't really blame him. This memoir gets three stars solely due to how fascinating it is to see what it takes to become the most successful Geiko* of a generation.**<br /><br /><i>Geisha of Gion</i> is far from a perfect read. My gripes, in no particular order:<br /><br />1) Other reviewers suggest that Iwasaki had a ghost writer. If she did, she was robbed. There was no natural flow between her anecdotes. One sensational incident after another, with little insight to how in made her feel or how it affected her life. <br /><br />2) I don't believe all of her stories. She claims to have slept only three hours a night for years on end. She claims to of had a premonition of a friend's death. (She has a lot of foreboding premonitions that turn out to be spot on.) This memoir begins when she is three years old. She claims to have an acute memory but it's a bit too much to swallow. I can't remember what happened last week as clearly as she recounts the events of her early childhood. <br /><br />3) Her eldest sister (literally and symbolically) Yaeko is a cartoon villain. She must of been seriously unhinged. Why did the mother of the household hand her such an important responsibility over Mineko? It is never explained. <br /><br />4) Mineko Iwasaki is easy to dislike. Being groomed from a young age to be a heiress must warp your personality somewhat, I'll admit. She was such a precocious child (and precocious adult) and she can't understand why she has no friends. She is often self-important and sanctimonious. When she is sitting next to Queen Elizabeth II at a dinner Iwasaki notices that she doesn't touch her dinner:<br /><br /><b>"I always try to eat whatever my host has been kind enough to serve me. To refuse would be discourteous and, if I were a visitor of state, it could even be construed as an affront to the nation, to say nothing of all the people who have worked so hard to prepare the meal."</b><br /><br />Iwasaki avenges her nation (and the chef) by flirting with the Duke of Edinburgh. Cut her some slack, Mineko. She's just flown half way around the world to be at some boring state dinner. Japanese cuisine is an acquired taste. Especially in the 1970's when even sushi was completely exotic to the British palette. <br /><br />5) Iwasaki always happens to be the best at everything and will tell you so herself. She takes up golf:<br /><b>"I took private lessons for a few weeks and was soon scoring in the 80s and 90s. No one could believe it"</b><br /><br />Whenever she visits Gion: <b>"When I tell them my name is Mineko, they invariably fly into a tizzy and ask, 'Are you the real Mineko? The legend? It is wonderful to spend time with them."</b><br /><br />No wonder all her peers had tall poppy syndrome.<br /><br />6) While she goes into some detail about her dancing and the importance of kimono, sometimes she just throws a piece of info at you without context. For instance, once she was adopted she suckled her elder sister's breast to go to sleep... she continued to do this until she was over 10 years old... excuse me? Was this normal in Japan at that time? Or was it unique to Mineko? Why did everyone in your adopted family just go with it? Some elaboration would have been fantastic. At another point, she goes to get her face shaved which she has done regularly since she was a child. Is this still done in Japan? To what end?<br /><br />Mineko Iwasaki is a formidable woman and her achievements are extraordinary. I just wish her tale was in the hands of an experienced biographer who could breathe some life into her story.<br /><br />*Geisha can refer to men or women and simply means artist. In Kyoto, the term Geiko is used to describe what those in the West would describe as a Geisha. <br /><br />** It also gets three stars because <i>
  <b>boo</b>
</i> the patriarchy. <i>
  <b>Yay!</b>
</i> for women telling their own stories.</span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1544672502?book_show_action=true">Feb 10, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brittany" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brittany" href="/user/show/30535238-brittany">Brittany</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30535238-brittany?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30535238-brittany?shelf=reviews">reviews</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1544672502" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8064733295014193102"><a href='http://kiribear.blogspot.com' rel='nofollow noopener'>Check out my blog to see Reviews of Books and Movies as well as Recipes and DIY projects</a><br/><br/>This book was a solid 4 star read for me. Whereas Arthur Golden's Memoirs of a Geisha was meant to entertain, Mineko Isawaki's Geisha, a Life was meant to inform.<br/><br/>Mineko Isawaki is most notable for being one of, if not THE most famous Geisha in Japan's history. This autobiography is told from her own view of the traditions and trails that she faced. <br/><br/>Mineko does a brilliant job of taking the reader through the </span>
  <span id="freeText8064733295014193102" style="display:none"><a href="http://kiribear.blogspot.com" rel="nofollow noopener">Check out my blog to see Reviews of Books and Movies as well as Recipes and DIY projects</a><br /><br />This book was a solid 4 star read for me. Whereas Arthur Golden's Memoirs of a Geisha was meant to entertain, Mineko Isawaki's Geisha, a Life was meant to inform.<br /><br />Mineko Isawaki is most notable for being one of, if not THE most famous Geisha in Japan's history. This autobiography is told from her own view of the traditions and trails that she faced. <br /><br />Mineko does a brilliant job of taking the reader through the grueling daily schedule she had from a young age as well as giving a lot of historical background to her life and the life of a Geiko (female artist). She dispels much of the rumors of geishas being little more than pleasure companions. I love the detail she gives on traditions of a geiko as well as the intricacies associated with each year and season and the symbolism and immense cost of each important occasion and dress of a geisha's career. <br /><br />This book is drastically different from memoirs of a Geisha and makes you wonder how both of these stories are about the same thing. This is definitely not a re-read of Memoirs of a Geisha and stands on its own If you are interested in learning more about geisha, and as known in Kyoto as geiko, I highly recommend giving this book a read. </span>
  <a data-text-id="8064733295014193102" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/10697228?book_show_action=true">Dec 19, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tracey" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tracey" href="/user/show/349264-tracey">Tracey</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

            Shelves:

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer10697228" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6041953148069505678">I'd vaguely remembered hearing/reading something (maybe on NPR or 50bookchallenge posts) about Mineko Iwasaki, the prime source & inspiration for Arthur Golden's <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/search/search?q=Memoirs%20of%20a%20Geisha:%20A%20Novel' title='Memoirs of a Geisha: A Novel' rel='nofollow noopener'>Memoirs of a Geisha: A Novel</a>, being disappointed with the portrayal of the geisha life in that novel, and therefore, she had written her own memoirs. So I checked this book out from the library and I now see where her concerns lie. <br/><br/>Mineko (born Masako Tanaka) joined the Iwasaki okiya as a child, due to some family issues. She was fascin</span>
  <span id="freeText6041953148069505678" style="display:none">I'd vaguely remembered hearing/reading something (maybe on NPR or 50bookchallenge posts) about Mineko Iwasaki, the prime source &amp; inspiration for Arthur Golden's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/search/search?q=Memoirs%20of%20a%20Geisha:%20A%20Novel" title="Memoirs of a Geisha: A Novel" rel="nofollow noopener">Memoirs of a Geisha: A Novel</a>, being disappointed with the portrayal of the geisha life in that novel, and therefore, she had written her own memoirs. So I checked this book out from the library and I now see where her concerns lie. <br /><br />Mineko (born Masako Tanaka) joined the Iwasaki okiya as a child, due to some family issues. She was fascinated by the dance, striving to reach perfection as the only way to make everything right. She debuted as a minarai, or apprentice geisha, at an unusually young age and worked herself nearly to death for the next few years. As she matured, she became one of the most popular geisha of the Gion area. Eventually, she started to burn out and made the decision to end her career rather early. Falling in love may have had something to do with her decision as well. <br /><br />The two sections of pictures were very helpful to identify certain elements of the dress, as well as get a feel for the flow of her life. The writing style is relatively plain and straightforward, what I would expect of an autobiography, as opposed to a work of fiction. Some of the fine points of the social standings and rituals got a little confusing, however. <br /><br />It was interesting to read these two books back to back - seeing what small incidences in Iwasaki's life Golden chose to modify into major plot elements, and vice versa. For example, Golden chose to move his story back one generation (Iwasaki was born in 1949), probably in order to add WWII as a dramatic plot point. The attribution of a certain ritual of the oiran (courtesans) to the geishas was probably what made Iwasaki upset. (and I wouldn't blame her!) <br /><br />Recommended to anyone looking for a more realistic portrayal of the waning "flower and willow world" of the geisha.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6041953148069505678" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_630080054" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/630080054?book_show_action=true">Jun 01, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kim-Lost-In-A-Book" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kim-Lost-In-A-Book" href="/user/show/1838870-kim-lost-in-a-book">Kim-Lost-In-A-Book</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1838870-kim-lost-in-a-book?shelf=2013-reads">2013-reads</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer630080054" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6779980611245179080">I thoroughly enjoyed this memoir. I think it's a very real glimpse into a world many know very little about (but like to think they know more than they do). I liked the insight to traditional Japanese culture, something I've been interested in since my youth. Mineko lead a life that most women can not comprehend, and many would probably find appalling or undesirable, but Mineko lived it well, I think. While she was naïve in many ways, in others she was quite strong and mindful of how best to han</span>
  <span id="freeText6779980611245179080" style="display:none">I thoroughly enjoyed this memoir. I think it's a very real glimpse into a world many know very little about (but like to think they know more than they do). I liked the insight to traditional Japanese culture, something I've been interested in since my youth. Mineko lead a life that most women can not comprehend, and many would probably find appalling or undesirable, but Mineko lived it well, I think. While she was naïve in many ways, in others she was quite strong and mindful of how best to handle some difficult situations. The misconceptions about geiko are long lived and sad. It's also sad that their very own culture feeds the misconceptions with similarities between the geiko and oiran "ceremonies". <br /><br />I think the geiko (geisha) life is very interesting and beautiful. It is, of course, not without its flaws. Like any other society or art form. When people are involved especially people of different personalities are involved there will always be discord in some way. Mineko's story showed me that even back in the "good old days" there were still people who did not take their jobs seriously. That they didn't value their heritage and the traditions of their jobs and cultures. That is something you could never say about Mineko. She certainly valued the traditions, even while trying to modernize and improve them (ie: more academic education for the girls especially in foreign language courses). She took her job very seriously and she expected others to as well. Mineko says herself that geiko is a dying art (for various reasons, economic not being the least of them). This is a sad fact to me. I would love to see it continue for many more generations. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6779980611245179080" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2294628885" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2294628885" />
    <a title="Juushika" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/616321-juushika"><img alt="Juushika" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1541586729p2/616321.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2294628885?book_show_action=true">Feb 13, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Juushika" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Juushika" href="/user/show/616321-juushika">Juushika</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/616321-juushika?shelf=genre-memoir-and-biography">genre-memoir-and-biography</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/616321-juushika?shelf=genre-non-fiction">genre-non-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/616321-juushika?shelf=status-borrowed">status-borrowed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2294628885" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1026785257594985459">The autobiography of Mineko Iwasaki, the most famous geisha in Japan until her sudden retirement at the height of her career. This is written partially in response to Arthur Golden's <i>Memoirs of a Geisha</i> (although it never says so directly); as such, it's made accessible to a foreign audience and does much to explain the controversy surrounding <i>Memoirs</i>, particularly the liberties that book takes with Iwasaki's life story, as well as the way it elides geisha and prostitution. This is also a memoir</span>
  <span id="freeText1026785257594985459" style="display:none">The autobiography of Mineko Iwasaki, the most famous geisha in Japan until her sudden retirement at the height of her career. This is written partially in response to Arthur Golden's <i>Memoirs of a Geisha</i> (although it never says so directly); as such, it's made accessible to a foreign audience and does much to explain the controversy surrounding <i>Memoirs</i>, particularly the liberties that book takes with Iwasaki's life story, as well as the way it elides geisha and prostitution. This is also a memoir in its own right. Iwasaki relies heavily on anecdotes; her memory is precise, her language evocative, her personality changeable and occasionally smug. She simultaneously loves and criticizes the hierarchical social structure, restrictiveness, skill, artistry, and effort that contribute to a geisha's craft, particularly as interacts with gender and as it has failed to change with the times; her experience and opinions are fervent and complex. This throughline isn't as solid as it could be--in particular, it wants for a stronger conclusion, perhaps an argument about what she believes the future of geisha should look like. But it's a compelling effort, and especially valuable in a world where <i>Memoirs of a Geisha</i> is such a problematic and popular text.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1026785257594985459" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David Nicol" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David Nicol" href="/user/show/9494429-david-nicol">David Nicol</a>

        rated it

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer515959578" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer3552337597708846845">I really liked it for the peek inside the life of a meiko/geiko in post war Japan. Mineko herself as a child is what we in the West would call a precocious little brat, but is more of a misinterpretation of the class system.<br/><br/>Two things that were negatives for me though were the fact that either Iwasaki or Brown had never seen a Shamisen and/or a Viola.   <span id="freeText3552337597708846845" style="display:none">I really liked it for the peek inside the life of a meiko/geiko in post war Japan. Mineko herself as a child is what we in the West would call a precocious little brat, but is more of a misinterpretation of the class system.<br /><br />Two things that were negatives for me though were the fact that either Iwasaki or Brown had never seen a Shamisen and/or a Viola. The text states that a Shamisen is played like a Viola.... that I would like to see.<br /><br />The second thing was Mineko's assertion that she doesn't pass wind, or fart as we call it in these parts. Either it was to save face in front of her doctor, or she actually did believe that she hasn't farted in her whole life. The latter would explain why Toshio wouldn't leave his wife for her, he probably couldn't bear to spend the rest of his life with a woman who was perfect throughout the day, but would blow him across the futon at night with her trumpet bum.<br /><br />The whole story is pretty much a culture shock. But an enjoyable read if you have an interest in other cultures.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3552337597708846845" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Maria Elmvang" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/168347-maria-elmvang"><img alt="Maria Elmvang" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442612167p2/168347.jpg" /></a>

        <a title="Maria Elmvang" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Maria Elmvang" href="/user/show/168347-maria-elmvang">Maria Elmvang</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780743430593" href="/book/show/935.Geisha_of_Gion">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/168347-maria-elmvang?shelf=cultural">cultural</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/168347-maria-elmvang?shelf=2007">2007</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2680571" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15139955731261504559">Ever since I read "Memoirs of a Geisha" I've wanted to read this one, as Arthur Golden mentions this book as being one of his inspirations. On my way to Italy I found it at the airport, and immediately bought it. It did not disappoint. Where MoaG takes place around World War 2, this one describes the life of a Geisha in the 60s and 70s. You get to read about how Mineko meets Prince Charles, Queen Elizabeth and several other celebrities that we 'know'. Fascinating book.</span>

          </span>
        </div>
